  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f04146c8-b144-4f5f-b76a-c098375777e8'
      });
    }

  <span id="freeText4423749905935437762" style="display:none"><b>From the author of the bestselling <i>The Dangerous Book for Boys</i></b><br /><b>Â </b><br />With his acclaimed Emperor novels, author Conn Iggulden brings a dazzling world to lifeâthe rich, complex world of ancient Rome as seen through the eyes of one extraordinary man: Julius Caesar. Now Iggulden returns to the story of Julius Caesar and a realm that stretches from the sands of North Africa to the coast of Britain. Against this magnificent backdrop, Caesar, his first victories under his belt and a series of key alliances in place, makes his move toward power and gloryâand commands his famous legions on one of historyâs bloodiest and most daring military campaigns.<br /><br /> It is the heart of the first century B.C. For Julius Caesar, the time has come to enter the treacherous political battleground that has become Rome. Having proved his valor in the slavesâ revolt, Caesar is strengthened by the love and vision of a beautiful older woman, and by the sword of his loyal friend, Marcus Brutus. And when he is appointed to a new position of power, Caesar manages to do what none of the other great figures of his time could: capture the hearts of the Roman people themselves. Crushing a rebellion, bringing order to the teeming city, Caesar then makes the move that will change history. He leaves Rome for the foothills of the Alps. And with an army made in his own image, he begins a daring charge through Gaul, across the English Channel, and to the wilds of tribal Britain.<br /><br /> Here, in a series of cataclysmic clashes, the legend of Julius Caesar will be forged. And while Caesar and Brutus pit their livesâand those of their menâagainst the armies of the wilderness, their political adversaries in Rome grow at once more fearful and more formidable. So when the fighting at the dominionâs edge is over, the greatest danger to Julius Caesar will await him on the Tiberâwith a man who wants Rome himself.<br /><br /> From the clash of armies to the heat of a womanâs seduction, from the thunder of battle to the orgies of pleasure and plunder that follow in a warriorâs wake,<b> Emperor: The Field of Swords</b> captures in riveting detail a world being shaped by a brilliant civilization. And in this extraordinary novel, the fate of Rome is being driven by the ambitions of a single man. A man with an unmatched genius for power.<br /><br /><br /><i>From the Hardcover edition.</i></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2461055282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12565912569382157450">This author writes great Historical fiction. I've read 9 of his books and I always know what I'm in for when I settle into one. They are a real treat. He is usually in the 4-5 star range for me. This one, I liked, but I didn't love it as much as some of his others. But it did have the things that I've come to expect from the author. I loved the research and the way he seamlessly threads it all throughout. I sounds authentic and I liked the dialogue and the other characters. Even with all of that</span>
  <span id="freeText12565912569382157450" style="display:none">This author writes great Historical fiction. I've read 9 of his books and I always know what I'm in for when I settle into one. They are a real treat. He is usually in the 4-5 star range for me. This one, I liked, but I didn't love it as much as some of his others. But it did have the things that I've come to expect from the author. I loved the research and the way he seamlessly threads it all throughout. I sounds authentic and I liked the dialogue and the other characters. Even with all of that said, this one didn't grip me like the others, but still worth the read. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11054643873033337285">Perfect series for all who are intrigued and owed with the old Rome and great man that was Gajus Julius Ceasar.   <span id="freeText11054643873033337285" style="display:none">Perfect series for all who are intrigued and owed with the old Rome and great man that was Gajus Julius Ceasar. Conn Iggulden is taking us through the life, tastes, smells, blood and politics that shaped the world we know today. Cesar was a brilliant man in a cruel world and with cruelty that matched that same world with ease. At the same time, he was loved by the ones he turned into the deadliest force the world saw even long after he left this world. These books also show us how hard life was back then, how cheap that same life was, how politics change even purest hearts, how devastating wars are, and only true genius survive all of that bringing a new life, for better or worse. Like one critic said, read it before they make a movie. Brilliant. Powerful.True. For better or worse. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73997789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3987220060220422787">From Spain to Gaul to Britain and back to Rome<br/><br/>This is the third book in the series of four and continues at tremendous pace. Caesar is posted to Spain by his political rivals who, rightly, see him as a threat.<br/><br/>He is successful in his Iberian adventure, but returns to Rome in time for the consular elections. Following his election to high office, the means of which is highly amusing, he cannot sit still for long and soon leaves Crassus and Pompey in charge so that he can take his armies to Gaul to</span>
  <span id="freeText3987220060220422787" style="display:none">From Spain to Gaul to Britain and back to Rome<br /><br />This is the third book in the series of four and continues at tremendous pace. Caesar is posted to Spain by his political rivals who, rightly, see him as a threat.<br /><br />He is successful in his Iberian adventure, but returns to Rome in time for the consular elections. Following his election to high office, the means of which is highly amusing, he cannot sit still for long and soon leaves Crassus and Pompey in charge so that he can take his armies to Gaul to spread the benefits of the Roman culture. Eventually, he makes it into Britain, but that ambitious move is just a stretch too far for the already extended Roman armies.<br /><br />Many new characters are introduced in this book, both friends an enemies. Some of these are destined to become very influential in the close circle that forms around the most powerful man in Europe of that time. Brutus starts to become dangerously jealous whenever Caesar shows any favour to others, such as Mark Anthony. You can see where it's headed as the story steams at full speed towards the fourth, and final, book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3987220060220422787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3083860417978653021">I planned to have a break from this series having read the first three volumes in quick succession, however this ends on such a cliff hanger that I feel I have to carry on with volume four very soon <br/><br/>I enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330947.The_Gates_of_Rome__Emperor___1_' title='The Gates of Rome (Emperor, #1) by Conn Iggulden' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Gates of Rome</a>, the first volume of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/119121.Conn_Iggulden' title='Conn Iggulden' rel='nofollow noopener'>Conn Iggulden</a>âs sequence of five novels about Julius Caesar, so much that I pressed straight on with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/671724.The_Death_of_Kings__Emperor___2_' title='The Death of Kings (Emperor, #2) by Conn Iggulden' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Death of Kings</a>, the second novel, and then onto this, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330944.The_Field_of_Swords__Emperor___3_' title='The Field of Swords (Emperor, #3) by Conn Iggulden' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Field of Swords</a> (Emperor #3).<br/><br/>Once again, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/119121.Conn_Iggulden' title='Conn Iggulden' rel='nofollow noopener'>Conn Iggulden</a> has penned a page turner all of wh</span>
  <span id="freeText3083860417978653021" style="display:none">I planned to have a break from this series having read the first three volumes in quick succession, however this ends on such a cliff hanger that I feel I have to carry on with volume four very soon <br><br>I enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330947.The_Gates_of_Rome__Emperor___1_" title="The Gates of Rome (Emperor, #1) by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gates of Rome</a>, the first volume of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/119121.Conn_Iggulden" title="Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">Conn Iggulden</a>âs sequence of five novels about Julius Caesar, so much that I pressed straight on with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/671724.The_Death_of_Kings__Emperor___2_" title="The Death of Kings (Emperor, #2) by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Death of Kings</a>, the second novel, and then onto this, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330944.The_Field_of_Swords__Emperor___3_" title="The Field of Swords (Emperor, #3) by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Field of Swords</a> (Emperor #3).<br><br>Once again, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/119121.Conn_Iggulden" title="Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">Conn Iggulden</a> has penned a page turner all of which is rooted in fact. Once again, the historical note at the bookâs conclusion explains what happened and where the narrative veers from the record.<br><br>Julius Caesar is such a wonderful character for this kind of treatment. In this instalment he has takes his legions north to battle with the Gallic tribes for domination of Gaul, and even starts subduing the Britannia tribes. Meanwhile back in Rome there are the usual political shenanigans raging. The book ends in 53 BC with Julius and his battle-hardened legions poised to cross the Rubicon river and attack Rome and dictator Pompey. <br><br>Once again <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330944.The_Field_of_Swords__Emperor___3_" title="The Field of Swords (Emperor, #3) by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Field of Swords</a> scores highly on its depiction of the minutiae of Roman lives.<br><br>Perhaps not quite as gripping as the first two books, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330944.The_Field_of_Swords__Emperor___3_" title="The Field of Swords (Emperor, #3) by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Field of Swords</a> is still a rip roaring read.<br><br>4/5<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1388160940l/11431285.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11820471.The_Death_of_Kings__Emperor___2_" title="The Death of Kings (Emperor, #2) by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Death of Kings</a> (2004) by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/119121.Conn_Iggulden" title="Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">Conn Iggulden</a></span>

<div id="review_417969149" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417969149" />
    <a title="Steve " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4121048-steve"><img alt="Steve " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395963300p2/4121048.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/417969149?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve " href="/user/show/4121048-steve">Steve </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007136933" href="/book/show/1265660.The_Field_Of_Swords">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4121048-steve?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer417969149" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2091194759217551531">I didn't enjoy this book as much as I enjoyed the previous two in this series. Why? Good question. For me, Caesar spends far too much of the books' emphasis on his campaigns out of Rome. Starting in Spain, then a really long time (much of the book) in Gaul, then his failures in Britain. I love historical fiction, and facts, but there was no way of knowing all the conversations and details of what happened in his long campaign in Gaul. That part dragged on too far for me, for too much of the book</span>
  <span id="freeText2091194759217551531" style="display:none">I didn't enjoy this book as much as I enjoyed the previous two in this series. Why? Good question. For me, Caesar spends far too much of the books' emphasis on his campaigns out of Rome. Starting in Spain, then a really long time (much of the book) in Gaul, then his failures in Britain. I love historical fiction, and facts, but there was no way of knowing all the conversations and details of what happened in his long campaign in Gaul. That part dragged on too far for me, for too much of the book and kept much of the historical politics of Rome from the book. Yes, Caesar had a very long campaign fighting the Gauls, but I don't want half a book on fictional minute details. The deal he struck with Crassus and Pompey was interesting, as were the battles. But much of Rome was detailed in the politics and thoughts of Crassus and Pompey, though quite accurate, I didn't buy a book to read so much of Crassus and Pompey. They're good, factual, characters and former historical leaders in their own devious right. <br /><br />But I would have prefered less of the book giving so much unknown, fictional, detail in Gaul. Then bringing our leading character (and Marcus) back to Rome much earlier to follow Caesar and his political struggles and achievements... That's just my personal opinion and preference, but I'm pretty sure I'd have given it at least '4 stars' for that. As it is, I liked the book. It was an enjoyable read. But, I didn't find it as enjoyable as the first two. It was a page-turner. But a page-turner where you hope Caesar gets back to Rome sooner. <br /><br />Historically, it follows on with Caesar's life. Though, I was left feeling as though this book - good as it is - was more of a 'filler' leading up to the next book, where you can 'feel' the 'gripping politics' and 'Caesar's senetorial prowess' is 'yet to come'. <br /><br />Hence, '3 stars' from me ;)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2091194759217551531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9959683064245748612">1995:Conn Iggulden complimented another author by saying that <i> âSome authors are better historians than they are storytellers. Anthony Riches is brilliant at both.â </i>. I think that this also describes Mr Iggulden's writing as well. If I wanted an exact historical rendition of Julius Caesar, I would pick up a relevant historical tome. But I happen to like my history told as a story with an explanation offered for some of the seemingly random choices made by people great and small. Many of the revi</span>
  <span id="freeText9959683064245748612" style="display:none">1995:Conn Iggulden complimented another author by saying that <i> âSome authors are better historians than they are storytellers. Anthony Riches is brilliant at both.â </i>. I think that this also describes Mr Iggulden's writing as well. If I wanted an exact historical rendition of Julius Caesar, I would pick up a relevant historical tome. But I happen to like my history told as a story with an explanation offered for some of the seemingly random choices made by people great and small. Many of the reviews that I have read about this book mention some possible historical omissions or incorrect time lines. Well, the only thing that I really knew about Julius Caesar is that he was betrayed by his friend on the Ides of March. This book has made me determined to actually delve a bit more into the factual story of Julius Caesar - so I have been inspired by this book and I am sure that many other readers would be too. For my tastes, the balance between the "softer" emotional passages and the hard battle sequences was just right. HIghly recommended.</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7496175357724088903">Taking up the story where the last one left off... this is the third book in the four book Emperor series.<br/><br/>We finally meet Mark Antony... Caesar, Crassus and Pompey form their Triumvirate... we lose some favourites... fight more battles.... and face more duplicity than you'd expect to find at a Two-timers get together....<br/><br/>Wonderful... </span>

  <span id="freeText378763615813077578" style="display:none">Welcome back to the Emperor series. At the end of The Death of Kings, we saw Caesar ordered off to Spain by Pompey after his successful exploits in Africa, Greece and later against Spartacus. Caesar is dissatisfied in Spain and believes his time to return Rome has come. <br /><br />For the first time I had to abandon a Conn audiobook, because the narration was dire. Fortunately, I have a friend who was willing to loan me his paperback copy so that my experience of the Emperor series wouldnât be damaged by having to listen to substandard narration (who calls Octavian âOc-tarh-vianâ).<br /><br />My complaint aside, book 3 was a very enjoyable read and because I had it on loan rather than owning a copy myself, I managed to finish it much quicker than I would normally. Once again Conn Iggulden has delivered a thoroughly gripping tale. There was no shortage of action here, with battles aplenty across modern France, Germany, Britain and even Rome itself.<br /><br />The politics took a very interesting turn, both when it came to Julius, Pompey and Crassus, as well as the political tensions between Julius, Brutus and Mark Anthony (who surprisingly I found to be quite a whinger). Brutus was very much a central character again which I was pleased about since he was side-lined for much of book 2, and I think the relationship Caesar had with his mother made things very intense at some points<br /><br />In all this one deserves 4.5 stars rounded up to 5, and after the cliff hanger ending I will be diving into book 4 immediately (also loaned to me because the audiobook for The Gods of War has the same narrator as this one). If you are fond of the Roman period of history and Julius Caesar and you havenât read this series, I strongly urge you to do so.<br /></span>

<div id="review_284092061" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/284092061" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1432709-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231228544p2/1432709.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/284092061?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/1432709-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432709-julie?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432709-julie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer284092061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10733400739074134577">My book review for <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.audiojukebox.com'>www.audiojukebox.com</a><br/><br/>Today is March 15th, or as most of us know, the Ides of March. The three most common facts known about Julius Caesar is that he was one of the greatest rulers of the Roman Empire, he was assassinated on the Ides of March, and he created an amazing salad dressing that he named after himself. Okay, so I made up that last bit about the salad dressing to see if anyone really reads these reviews. But if you are at all curious and want to learn more about the lif</span>
  <span id="freeText10733400739074134577" style="display:none">My book review for <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.audiojukebox.com">www.audiojukebox.com</a><br /><br />Today is March 15th, or as most of us know, the Ides of March. The three most common facts known about Julius Caesar is that he was one of the greatest rulers of the Roman Empire, he was assassinated on the Ides of March, and he created an amazing salad dressing that he named after himself. Okay, so I made up that last bit about the salad dressing to see if anyone really reads these reviews. But if you are at all curious and want to learn more about the life of Caesar, or even want to hear some thrilling historical fiction, then listen to Conn Igguldenâs <i>Emperor</i> series. There are four books to this amazing series that cover Caesarâs entire life starting as a young boy growing up in Ancient Rome through his rise to power as a military conqueror and dictator and finishing with his decline and assassination. Each book in this series is pretty lengthy â The Field of Swords weighs in at over 17 and a half hours, which might seem a bit of overkill in describing the life of any individual, even a type A overachiever like Julius Caesar. But Conn Igguldenâs books cover so much more than the mere life of one man; they provide a vivid storytelling experience that describes the people, the times and the amazing power of the Roman Empire. <br /><br /><i>The Field of Swords</i> begins in Spain where Caesar has led his legions on a very successful campaign that has captured the Iberian peninsula for Rome. Although his political enemies, feeling threatened by his status as a conqueror and hero, want him to stay away from the city, Julius decides to return to Rome and run for election as Consol. As with elections today, winning the support and votes of the people costs a small fortune, and the election leaves Caesar victorious, but heavily in debt. What is the best way to get out of debt? Conquer a neighboring territory, of course, and Caesar heads off to run a campaign in Gaul. The story alternates between Caesarâs military campaign in Gaul and Britain and the political intrigue and treachery back in Rome. Both parts are exciting and filled with many major historical players of the time â Pompey, Mark Antony, Crassius, and Brutus, as well as a large cast of colorful and well depicted fictional characters. I loved learning about the military strategy of the highly trained and disciplined Roman legions as well as the complex negotiations in the political arena, making this book not only highly entertaining, but definitely worth the listen for the history lesson.<br /><br />The audiobook is read by Paul Blake. This is my first experience with him as a narrator and I have to say that my feelings about this performance are mixed. His voices for various characters were satisfactory, but not distinct enough that I could tell one character from the other by simply hearing a piece of dialog. This did not really cause any confusion as far as the plot is concerned, but it made this performance feel not as polished. I also found his narrations of descriptive text passages to be overly emotional and over acted, which for me, detracted from the book. However, either he improved over time or I became accustomed to his style because after 5 or 6 hours into the book, I was hooked and found myself loving the story. <br /><br />So, whether you want an exciting novel of ancient Rome, or want to learn more about the great man, Julius Caesar, then Friends, Romans, and Countrymen â lend your ears to this audiobook and enjoy a captivating story. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10733400739074134577" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1392100163" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1466028185033089340"><br/>Events move at a fast clip throughout the book. The action never stops, which gives the reader a sense of the frenetic energy Julius Caesar feels inside him as he moves forward, not wanting to waste any time while trying to figure out his path. The reader is taken on a tour of Europe as Caesar dashes from Spain to Italy to France to Germany to England, creating a new world in his wake because he does not want to leave anything at status quo. <br/><br/>I loved that these characters canât tell the future. I</span>
  <span id="freeText1466028185033089340" style="display:none"><br />Events move at a fast clip throughout the book. The action never stops, which gives the reader a sense of the frenetic energy Julius Caesar feels inside him as he moves forward, not wanting to waste any time while trying to figure out his path. The reader is taken on a tour of Europe as Caesar dashes from Spain to Italy to France to Germany to England, creating a new world in his wake because he does not want to leave anything at status quo. <br /><br />I loved that these characters canât tell the future. It felt very real that all of the characters lived their lives day by day without making ominous speeches the day before something unexpected happens or ironic remarks regarding events that hadnât happened yet.<br /><br />Neither Julius Caesar nor anyone else here knows the history of things to come â which was very satisfying compared to a lot of historical fiction these days that goes overboard with foreshadowing and irony and broad winks to the audience. <br /><br />What also feels realistic here was that people recognized the talent Julius had and also that no matter what action he choose, it was going to have a large impact on those around him. <i>That</i> felt real â the way people around him braced themselves for him to cause big shake up by sheer force of will - but without a lot of muttering about prophecies.<br /><br />It ends on the cliffhanger of all cliffhangers (despite knowing the winners and losers of history) and leaves the reader dashing for the next book to see just how the known record is going to play out in this authorâs deft hands.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1148417403" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1148417403" />
    <a title="Arun Divakar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2747498-arun-divakar"><img alt="Arun Divakar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253201408p2/2747498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1148417403?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arun Divakar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arun Divakar" href="/user/show/2747498-arun-divakar">Arun Divakar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1148417403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9174490162834638027">As the story opens, Julius Caesar is wrapping up his adventures in Spain. With his ever ambitious and never silent mind driving him, he sets out to Rome. What follows is the creation of the first triumvirate with Pompey, Crassus and Caesar after which he sets course for the conquest of Gaul and thereby carving a place for himself in history. The curtains fall at the time when Caesar decides to lead his men across the Rubicon and back to Rome to challenge the might of Pompey.<br/><br/>Romans as I understan</span>
  <span id="freeText9174490162834638027" style="display:none">As the story opens, Julius Caesar is wrapping up his adventures in Spain. With his ever ambitious and never silent mind driving him, he sets out to Rome. What follows is the creation of the first triumvirate with Pompey, Crassus and Caesar after which he sets course for the conquest of Gaul and thereby carving a place for himself in history. The curtains fall at the time when Caesar decides to lead his men across the Rubicon and back to Rome to challenge the might of Pompey.<br /><br />Romans as I understand were proponents of this policy of 'my way or the highway' and in this they were pretty straightforward. In the early days of the Roman republic following the defeat of Hannibal, much energy was spent in expanding the horizons of the nation. And as history tells us no one has contributed more to that cause than Caesar with the Gallic war. The plot is one of tremendous importance to Roman history and Conn Iggulden does leave his mark as a fine writer of warfare. Barring a few portions of half baked romantic interludes and melodrama, the book is a no-nonsense and intensely fast paced one. This explains how I could finish a 600 page book in hardly three days. One contention of course is that Caesar's aspect of being a complete womanizer is rather overlooked here.<br /><br />Know your Roman history before you step in here ! If you are not careful, Conn Iggulden will have you believing that history was always so colorful as a Hollywood summer blockbuster.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9174490162834638027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="David Campton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6132768-david-campton"><img alt="David Campton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314356599p2/6132768.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202324457?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Campton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Campton" href="/user/show/6132768-david-campton">David Campton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6132768-david-campton?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202324457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16501984347366841397">I've consistently whined about the historical innacuracy in this series, but this one, apart from the core fiction that Caesar and Brutus shared much of their lives and careers together, sticks much more closely to the facts of Caesar's rise to the Consulship and his subsequent Gallic wars. Iggulden downplays some of the horrors of his subjugation of Gaul (such as watching the women and children of Alesia starve to death between the twin walls) but I suppose a lot of detail has to be sacrificed </span>
  <span id="freeText16501984347366841397" style="display:none">I've consistently whined about the historical innacuracy in this series, but this one, apart from the core fiction that Caesar and Brutus shared much of their lives and careers together, sticks much more closely to the facts of Caesar's rise to the Consulship and his subsequent Gallic wars. Iggulden downplays some of the horrors of his subjugation of Gaul (such as watching the women and children of Alesia starve to death between the twin walls) but I suppose a lot of detail has to be sacrificed in what was an action packed life, especially since some other readers have said they found this book too long. Perhaps this could have made two books, the first looking at his time in Spain and the consular campaign/triumvirate, and then one dedicated to the Gallic period alone. However, the broad historic fidelity of this book in contrast to the predecessors meant that I probably enjoyed this episode most, as I could enjoy Iggulden's story-telling skill without being narked by innaccuracies. The die is now cast and I can't wait to read the last episode...</span>
  <a data-text-id="16501984347366841397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11578521835540845570" style="display:none">Ah, Gaul. And Gaul. And more Gaul.<br /><br />Field of Swords is a bit battle-riffic for my preference. The first half of the book, full of politics and intrigue, is much more my style. The endless sword and horse and spear scenes made my eyes roll back in my head. Just for a minute, but over and over.<br /><br />I know, I know, it's a necessary part of Casear's history. And it's not even that I minded him being away from Rome, because watching him purge his grief in Spain worked for me. But there are only so many blood-soaked vistas I can comfortably contemplate.<br /><br />And it's not that the book isn't well-written. For what it is, it works. But I quickly lost track of the savage kings who kneel at Caesar's feet.<br /><br />Back to those who plot behind your back rather than point their weapons at you, please.</span>

<div id="review_539631279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/539631279" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11939474-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345157508p2/11939474.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/539631279?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/11939474-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer539631279&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer539631279" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15201268867461538087">Having smashed up Spartacus at the end of the last book, The Field of Swords sees Caesar start off in Spain as Governor, before going on to pulling his mate's mum, (illegally) returning to home to Rome early to challenge for the role of Consul, setting up the First Triumvirate, smashing his way through Gaul and Britain, before deciding whether to return home once again by order of his only remaining military rival (and previous ally) or whether to start a massive civil war.<br/><br/>Like with the last boo</span>
  <span id="freeText15201268867461538087" style="display:none">Having smashed up Spartacus at the end of the last book, The Field of Swords sees Caesar start off in Spain as Governor, before going on to pulling his mate's mum, (illegally) returning to home to Rome early to challenge for the role of Consul, setting up the First Triumvirate, smashing his way through Gaul and Britain, before deciding whether to return home once again by order of his only remaining military rival (and previous ally) or whether to start a massive civil war.<br /><br />Like with the last book, if you ignore the historical inaccuracies (which Iggulden acknowledges and discusses at the end of the book) and treat the book as a work of historical fiction (kind of based on fact), this is a fantastic read. Engrossing, very readable and defo does not disappoint.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15201268867461538087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_63134290" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63134290" />
    <a title="Hollis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1862015-hollis"><img alt="Hollis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401306542p2/1862015.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63134290?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hollis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hollis" href="/user/show/1862015-hollis">Hollis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007136933" href="/book/show/1265660.The_Field_Of_Swords">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1862015-hollis?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63134290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4502131311974236913">With two novels behind him, Iggulden is definitely into his stride by now as he writes this. It was just as action-packed and entertaining, but the prose was much less blocky and the technical use of language much better. I'm glad that the violence was toned down as well: I felt like the violence and brutality in the first two books was mindless at times and it often hampered my enjoyment.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2469424208" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2469424208" />
    <a title="Chuck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10045735-chuck"><img alt="Chuck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441083052p2/10045735.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2469424208?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chuck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chuck" href="/user/show/10045735-chuck">Chuck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2469424208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14605318984859332406">This is the story of Julius Caesar from when he left Spain for Rome, until he had conquered Gaul and was on the banks of the Rubicon. Caesar was a dynamic and pivotal historic figure, so this could have been an engaging novel, but sadly it wasn't. The author's understanding of his characters was shallow, didn't seem to know how build narrative tension. As a result, the book was just plain dull.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+FeetCYrhtn3CGIWRqHDF+j/KAmUickAZc8Obyh5rFKiWJmKmzaEf9H6c1XwzCUghEgIFw1ag6kn8EqQM2MisQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

It was fought by two badly matched armies that met in atrocious conditions on St Crispin\'s Day 1415, and resulted in an extraordinary victory that was celebrated in England long before Shakespeare immortalised it in Henry V. It has always been held to be the triumph of the longbow against the armoured knight, and of the common man against the feudal aristocrat, but those are history\'s myths. Bernard Cornwell, who has long wanted to write this story, depicts the reality behind the myths.\n\nNicholas Hook is an English archer. He seems born to trouble and, when his lord orders him to London as part of a force sent to quell an expected Lollard uprising, Nick\'s headstrong behaviour leads to him being proscribed an outlaw. He finds refuge across the Channel, part of an English mercenary force protecting the town of Soissons against the French. What happened at the Siege of Soissons shocked all Europe, and propels Nick back to England where he is enrolled in the archer companyof the doughty Sir John Cornwaille, a leader of Henry V\'s army. The army was superb, but sickness and the unexpected French defiance at Harfleur, reduce it to near-shambolic condition. Henry stubbornly refuses to accept defeat and, in appalling weather, leads his shrunken force to what appears to be inevitable disaster.\n\nAzincourt culminates in the battle. Seen from several points of view on the English side, but also from the French ranks, the scene is vivid, convincing and compelling. Bernard Cornwell has a great understanding of men at war and battlefields and this is his masterpiece. This is what it must have been like to fight at Agincourt.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16066910462790335526\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n   <span id="freeText8646791235878499349" style="display:none"><i>Also publishes under author name <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16155974.C_F__Iggulden" title="C.F. Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">C.F. Iggulden</a>.</i><br /><br />I was born in the normal way in 1971, and vaguely remember half-pennies and sixpences. I have written for as long as I can remember: poetry, short stories and novels. Itâs what I always wanted to do and read English at London University with writing in mind. I taught English for seven years and was Head of English at St. Gregoryâs RC High School in London by the end of that period. I have enormous respect for those who still labour at the chalk-face. In truth, I canât find it in me to miss the grind of paperwork and initiatives. I do miss the camaraderie of the smokersâ room, as well as the lessons where their faces lit up as they understood what I was wittering on about.<br /><br />My mother is Irish and from an early age she told me history as an exciting series of stories â with dates. My great-grandfather was a Seannachie, so I suppose story-telling is in the genes somewhere. My father flew in Bomber Command in WWII, then taught maths and science. Perhaps crucially, he also loved poetry and cracking good tales. Though it seems a dated idea now, I began teaching when boys were told only girls were good at English, despite the great names that must spring to mind after that statement. My father loved working with wood and equations, but he also recited âVitai Lampadaâ with a gleam in his eye and that matters, frankly.<br /><br />Iâve always loved historical fiction as a genre and cut my teeth on Hornblower and Tai-Pan, Flashman, Sharpe and Jack Aubrey. I still remember the sheer joy of reading my first Patrick OâBrian book and discovering there were nineteen more in the series. I love just about anything by David Gemmell, or Peter F. Hamilton or Wilbur Smith. I suppose the one thing that links all those is the love of a good tale.<br /><br />Thatâs about it for the moment. If youâd like to get in touch with me leave a comment in the forum or you can tweet me @Conn_Iggulden. Iâll leave it there for the moment. If youâve read my books, you know an awful lot about the way I think already. Thereâs no point overdoing it.<br /><br />Conn Iggulden<br /></span>

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_330947'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/330947.The_Gates_of_Rome?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Gates of Rome (Emperor, #1)<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/119121.Conn_Iggulden?origin=series_works\">Conn Iggulden<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.17 avg rating &mdash; 22,850 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2002\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer852090126044082292\">Rarely, if ever, does a new writer dazzle us with such a vivid imagination and storytelling, flawlessly capturing the essence of a land, a people, a legend. Conn Iggulden is just such a writer, bringing to vivid life one of the most fascinating eras <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText852090126044082292\" style=\"display:none\">Rarely, if ever, does a new writer dazzle us with such a vivid imagination and storytelling, flawlessly capturing the essence of a land, a people, a legend. Conn Iggulden is just such a writer, bringing to vivid life one of the most fascinating eras in human history. In a true masterpiece of historical fiction, Iggulden takes us on a breathtaking journey through ancient Rome, sweeping us into a realm of tyrants and slaves, of dark intrigues and seething passions. What emerges is both a grand romantic tale of coming-of-age in the Roman Empire and a vibrant portrait of the early years of a man who would become the most powerful ruler on earth: Julius Caesar. On the lush Italian peninsula, a new empire is taking shape. At its heart is the city of Rome, a place of glory and decadence, beauty and bloodshed. Against this vivid backdrop, two boys are growing to manhood, dreaming of battles, fame, and glory in service of the mightiest empire the world has ever known. One is the son of a senator, a boy of privilege and ambition to whom much has been given and from whom much is expected. The other is a bastard child, a boy of strength and cunning, whose love for his adoptive family-and his adoptive brother-will be the most powerful force in his life. As young Gaius and Marcus are trained in the art of combat-under the tutelage of one of Rome\'s most fearsome gladiators-Rome itself is being rocked by the art of treachery and ambition, caught in a tug-of-war as two rival generals, Marius and Sulla, push the empire toward civil war. For Marcus, a bloody campaign in Greece will become a young soldier\'s proving ground. For Gaius, the equally deadly infighting of the Roman Senate will be the battlefield where he hones his courage and skill. And for both, the love of an extraordinary slave girl will be an honor each will covet but only one will win. The two friends are forced to walk different paths, and by the time they meet again everything will have changed. Both will have known love, loss, and violence. And the land where they were once innocent will be thrust into the grip of bitter conflict-a conflict that will set Roman against Roman...and put their friendship to the ultimate test. Brilliantly interweaving history and adventure, Conn Iggulden conjures a stunning array of contrasts-from the bloody stench of a battlefield to the opulence of the greatest city in history, from the tenderness of a lover to the treachery of an assassin. Superbly rendered, grippingly told, Emperor, The Gates of Rome is a work of vaulting imagination from a powerful new voice in historical fiction. &quot;From the Hardcover edition.&quot;<\/span>\n  <a This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
